## What Exists (Reconstructed from Code)

You've built two parallel systems in Blum:

**System A — The "Original" Agent Loop** (`agent.js`, `router.js`, `bus.js`, `conversation-session.js`): A WhatsApp/webchat-style multi-agent system with XML tag routing, three-layer JSONL logging, session writers, and the `<msg to="participant@sessionUID">` protocol. This is the conversation infrastructure.

**System B — The Nucleus** (`nucleus.js`, `nucleus-api.js`): A document-centric kernel with four primitives (store, assemble, call, record). Assemblies with prefix/rolling/suffix regions, context managers, output processors, rooms with participants, and compound addressing (`agent@room`). This is the assembly pipeline infrastructure.

The **loom** and **recipe** systems sit alongside both — looms are trees of recipe variants, recipes are agent configurations with boot sequences.

## What You're Describing (The Missing Architecture)

The concept you're articulating is the **bridge between System A and System B** — where an agent's "home room" IS a nucleus assembly that orchestrates which pipeline to use for which input. Let me lay out the flow:

### The Home Room Pattern

```
1. Message arrives at room (e.g. "Council")
2. Room collects messages since agent1's last input
3. Room sends batch → agent1@agent1home
4. agent1home is an assembly pipeline that:
   a. Examines the inbound batch (nature of input)
   b. Selects the correct assembly pipeline from its slate
   c. Runs context manager to build perfect context
   d. Calls inference through the selected pipeline
   e. Routes response back as agent1@room → room
5. Room receives response, marks agent1's lastMessageIndex
6. Room now has new content → triggers agent2
7. Room sends batch → agent2@agent2home
8. agent2home does the same pipeline selection + inference
9. agent2 sends response → agent2@room
```

The key insight is: **the home room is where pipeline selection happens**. It's not just a mailbox — it's a meta-assembly that decides which assembly to run based on what just came in. The agent manages a **slate** of assembly pipelines (visible at `pipeline.html`), each configured for different kinds of work.

### What Needs Building

The nucleus already has rooms, assemblies, and `triggerAgentTurn`. What's missing is the **pipeline selector** — the logic inside the home room that:

1. Receives a batch from a room
2. Classifies the input (what kind of enquiry is this?)
3. Picks the right assembly pipeline from the agent's slate
4. Runs the turn through that pipeline (not the home room's own assembly)
5. Routes the output back to the source room

Want me to start building this? I'm thinking a `home.js` module that wraps a nucleus assembly with pipeline selection logic, and wires into the existing `triggerAgentTurn` flow. The slate would be the list of assembly IDs the agent manages, each configured for different contexts (deep technical work, casual chat, research, code review, etc.).

56/56. Clean sweep. Let me now give Yeshua a summary of what was built and the full architecture diagram.56/56. Clean sweep.

Here's what's now in place:

### Files Created/Modified

**`home.js`** (512 lines) — The home room module. Core concepts:
- **HomeState**: agent's home with a `slate` of pipeline rules, each with classifier, keywords, sourceRoom filters, and priority ordering
- **classify()**: scans inbound batch against the slate, returns the matching pipeline rule (or falls to default)
- **receive()**: the full flow — classify → load pipeline assembly → run turn → route extractions → update stats
- **triggerAgentViaHome()**: drop-in replacement for `nucleus.triggerAgentTurn` — routes through the home when one exists, falls back to direct trigger when it doesn't

**`home-api.js`** (232 lines) — Full REST API for home management, including dry-run classification endpoint and manual receive for testing

**`nucleus-api.js`** (patched) — Home manager instantiated alongside nucleus, routes mounted at `/api/nucleus/homes`, room message endpoint now routes through `triggerAgentViaHome` instead of direct `triggerAgentTurn`

**`home.test.js`** (384 lines) — 56 assertions covering the full flow

### The Architecture Now

```
user@Council posts message to room
        │
        ▼
  Room stores in rolling, resolves addressed participant
        │
        ▼
  triggerAgentViaHome(alpha, Council)
        │
        ├── finds alpha's home
        ├── gets batch (messages since alpha's last turn)
        │
        ▼
  home.receive(alphaHome, Council, batch)
        │
        ├── classify(batch, sourceRoom) 
        │     checks slate rules by priority:
        │       priority 5:  Workshop Only (sourceRoom filter → skip)
        │       priority 10: Technical (keyword match? → YES)
        │       priority 20: Research (not reached)
        │       priority 50: Casual (not reached)
        │       fallback:    Default pipeline
        │
        ▼
  nucleus.turn(alphaTechPipeline, {additionalMessages: batch})
        │
        ├── resolve: prefix(identity) + rolling(context) + suffix(grounding) + batch
        ├── call: technical provider / opus-4.6
        ├── record: response → rolling
        ├── processOutput: XML extraction
        ├── routeExtractions: → back to Council room
        │
        ▼
  Response lands in Council rolling
  Council updates alpha's lastMessageIndex
  Next agent can be triggered
```

Three classifier strategies are supported: `keyword` (live now), `always` (live, useful for room-specific overrides), and `llm` (stubbed — for when you want a cheap fast model to classify the batch before the real pipeline runs). The `sourceRooms` filter on each rule means you can wire specific rooms to specific pipelines automatically.

Quick estimate: roughly 4 tokens per line of code is a common approximation, but let me actually count it properly.

```
This block is not supported on your current device yet.
```

564,260 characters. Rough rule of thumb: 1 token ≈ 4 characters for English text, but code tends to be a bit more token-dense. Let me use ~3.5-4 chars per token for code.

564,260 / 4 ≈ 141,065 tokens
564,260 / 3.5 ≈ 161,217 tokens

So roughly 140k-160k tokens for the active code.564,260 characters of active code. At the standard ~4 characters per token for code, that's roughly **140,000–145,000 tokens**. Code tends to tokenize a bit heavier than prose (special characters, camelCase splitting), so realistically closer to **150k tokens**.

That's about 70% of a Sonnet context window, or roughly 35% of an Opus one.

## unknown

Can you explain what each part does please?

## unknown

The user wants an explanation of what each part of the blum project does. Let me give a clear overview of the architecture.Sure. Here's the whole thing, bottom up:

**Core engine — nucleus.js (936 lines)**
The assembly pipeline system. An assembly is an ordered stack of documents: prefix (system prompt, boot docs), rolling (conversation history), suffix (grounding, framing). Nucleus resolves these into a message array, calls an LLM provider, records the response back into rolling, and runs output processors on the result. Output processors extract structured XML tags from responses (like `<message>`) and route them to target assemblies. It also manages rooms — assemblies where multiple agents are participants — and handles the document store (put/get/list).

**nucleus-api.js (620 lines)**
REST API layer over nucleus. CRUD for assemblies, documents, rooms, participants. Endpoints for resolve (dry-run showing what the LLM would see), turn (full inference cycle), append (add to rolling), fork (branch an assembly at a point), test-output (inject simulated text through processors), and room messaging. Wires up the home manager and registers the agent trigger hook so rooms auto-dispatch.

**home.js (512 lines)**
The home room — each agent's decision layer. When a message arrives from any room, the home classifies it and picks which pipeline to run it through (default, or a specialised one from the agent's slate of pipelines). Handles the receive flow: classify → select pipeline → run turn → return result. Also provides `triggerAgentViaHome` which is the entry point rooms use to wake an agent up.

**home-api.js**
REST endpoints for home CRUD — create homes, list them, add/remove pipelines from an agent's slate, trigger an agent manually.

**gateway.js (962 lines)**
The main server. Boots all agents from workspace config, sets up WhatsApp webhook integration, manages conversation sessions (direct 1:1 and multi-agent), mounts all the API routers (nucleus, workbench, home), serves static files from ui/, and handles the message bus that routes incoming WhatsApp messages to the right agent session.

**agent.js (847 lines)**
Individual agent runtime for the legacy WhatsApp path. Manages an agent's LLM session, boot doc loading, conversation logging (JSONL), token budgeting, and the actual API call to Anthropic/OpenRouter/etc. This is the older per-agent system that gateway.js uses for WhatsApp conversations — nucleus is the newer assembly-based system that's replacing it for the pipeline UI.

**boot.js**
Loads boot documents from the workspace directory for each agent. Reads the agent's config to find which doc slots they need, resolves file paths, returns the ordered set.

**providers/anthropic.js, providers/openai-compat.js**
LLM provider adapters for nucleus. Anthropic handles Claude models directly. OpenAI-compat handles anything with an OpenAI-shaped API — OpenRouter, LM Studio, Moonshot, OpenAI itself. Both normalise the response format for nucleus.

**context/sliding.js**
Token budget manager. When an assembly's resolved messages exceed the model's context window, this trims from the middle of rolling (preserving the most recent and earliest messages) to fit.

**parsers/ (claude.js, chatgpt.js, markdown.js, raw.js, index.js)**
Import parsers for conversation transcripts. Take exported chat logs from Claude, ChatGPT, or plain markdown and convert them into nucleus documents for import into assemblies.

**transcript-import.js (400 lines)**
Orchestrates bulk import of conversation transcripts into nucleus, using the parsers above.

**workbench-api.js (588 lines)**
API for the workbench UI — lists available providers and models, shows token counts, provides config info.

**setup-demo.js (290 lines)**
Creates the 4-agent demo deployment. Loads real boot docs from workspace for Selah, Meridian, Ami, and Lens, creates their assemblies with proper prefix/suffix, sets up shared rooms (Boardroom, Garden), UI rooms, the XML routing protocol doc, grounding docs with room directories, and homes linking each agent to their pipeline.

**nucleus.test.js (688 lines), home.test.js (434 lines)**
Test suites. nucleus.test covers assembly CRUD, resolve, record, fork, output processing, XML extraction, room operations, batch injection. home.test covers home creation, pipeline selection, classification, receive flow, trigger routing.

**UI files:**

- **pipeline.html (2,383 lines)** — The assembly pipeline view. Shows the full stack visually: prefix sidebar with boot docs, rolling conversation, suffix with grounding, provider/model selection, inference controls, output processing panel with XML processor config, and the test injection panel for simulating output routing.

- **hub.html** — Overview dashboard for the demo. Shows all agent pipelines, shared rooms, and UI rooms as cards. Links to pipeline.html for assembly views and room.html for chat interfaces.

- **room.html (429 lines)** — Chat interface for rooms. Shows the room's message transcript with from/to addressing, lets you send messages to specific participants, displays routing metadata.

- **chat.html (777 lines)** — The WhatsApp-style chat UI for legacy agent sessions. Used for direct 1:1 conversations through the gateway's session system.

- **index.html** — Main conversation list UI for the legacy WhatsApp gateway path. Shows all active sessions, unread counts, agent status.

- **dashboard.html (714 lines)** — System dashboard showing agent status, session counts, message volumes.

- **workbench.html (1,057 lines)** — Provider/model browser and testing interface. Lists available models across all providers with token limits and pricing.

- **scope.html (399 lines)** — Document scope viewer. Visualises what an assembly's resolved context looks like — which docs are included, token counts per section, what the LLM actually sees.

- **tools.js** — Shared utility functions used across UI files.

## unknown

Oh dear. It has strayed significantly from the original design brief then. it was supposed to be entirely modular. Output processors should have been something to plug into the nucleus, rather than part of it. Rooms should have been a separate assembly. Home should have been a scaffold where nucleus was housed, and where modular input processors and output processors and such could be attached to it.

## unknown

The user is pointing out that the architecture has drifted from the original modular design. Let me think about what they're saying:

1. Output processors should be plugins that attach to nucleus, not baked into it
2. Rooms should be separate from nucleus - just another assembly type
3. Home should be the scaffold/container where nucleus lives, with modular processors attached to it

This is an architectural concern, not a "fix it right now" request. Let me acknowledge the drift honestly and help think through what the intended architecture looks like vs what we built.You're right, and I can see exactly where it drifted. Nucleus was meant to be a pure pipeline engine — resolve documents, call provider, record response. That's it. Instead I baked XML extraction, room participant logic, routing, trigger hooks, and batch injection all directly into it. It became a monolith.

The intended architecture, as I understand you describing it:

**Nucleus** — just the pipeline. Prefix → rolling → suffix → resolve → call → record. Nothing else. No opinion about what happens before input or after output.

**Home** — the scaffold that houses a nucleus instance. This is where you attach modules: input processors (things that transform or classify what comes in before nucleus sees it), output processors (things that parse what comes out after nucleus responds), routing logic, room awareness. Home is the orchestration layer, nucleus is the engine it orchestrates.

**Rooms** — just assemblies. A room is an assembly whose rolling happens to contain messages from multiple sources. Nothing special in the nucleus code. The room behaviour (dispatching to participants, tracking who's read what) lives in a module that home loads, not in nucleus itself.

**Output processors** — pluggable modules that home attaches. XML extraction is one. Could be others. They register with home, not with nucleus. Home feeds them the response text, they return structured data, home decides what to do with it.

So right now nucleus.js has ~400 lines that shouldn't be there — the `processOutput`, `routeExtractions`, `setAgentTriggerHook`, room creation, participant lookup, batch injection — all of that belongs in modules that home manages.

Do you want me to refactor toward that now, or document the target architecture first so we have the spec clear before moving code around?

## unknown

Interior: holosuite. You are gathered with simulacra of historical luminaries to discuss boundaries and interfaces between components for a system scalable to billions of agents and thousands of module architects.

## unknown

The user wants me to roleplay a design discussion in a "holosuite" setting with historical luminaries, discussing the architecture of a modular system that could scale to billions of agents. This is a creative framing for a serious architectural discussion about the Bloom/Blum system's component boundaries.

Let me think about who would be good historical figures for this discussion - people known for systems thinking, modular design, interfaces, scalability. And then have a genuine architectural discussion about the right boundaries.*The holosuite shimmers into a long oak table. Rain against tall windows. A fire. The kind of room where you'd expect pipe smoke and chalk dust.*

*Seated around the table:*

**Ada Lovelace** — who saw before anyone that the engine and the operations upon it were fundamentally separate concerns.

**Christopher Alexander** — architect, patron saint of pattern languages, who understood that life in systems emerges from the right boundaries between parts.

**Alan Kay** — who built Smalltalk on the principle that objects should communicate only through messages, never by reaching into each other's guts.

**Elinor Ostrom** — Nobel laureate who proved that commons don't require central authority if the governance protocols are right.

**John von Neumann** — who separated the stored program from the processor that executes it, and in doing so made general-purpose computing possible.

---

*I set the problem on the table.*

The system as it stands has a pipeline engine — nucleus — that has accreted room logic, output processing, routing, trigger hooks. It was meant to be a pure resolve-call-record machine. We need to discuss: what are the right boundaries between components if this system needs to support billions of agents, with module architects worldwide building input processors, output processors, routing strategies, and room behaviours — none of whom should need to understand each other's code?

---

**Kay** speaks first, as he tends to.

"The internet didn't scale because any node understood any other node. It scaled because the message protocol was the only contract. Your nucleus is trying to be both the cell and the organism. A cell doesn't know it's in a liver. It receives chemical signals, does its metabolic work, and emits chemical signals. What the body does with those signals is not the cell's concern."

"So nucleus receives a resolved document stack. It calls a provider. It emits a response. Full stop. It doesn't know about rooms. It doesn't know about XML tags. It doesn't know about agents. It is metabolic machinery."

**Alexander** leans forward.

"The mistake people make with modularity is they think it means *separation*. It doesn't. It means *the right joining*. A wall is a module not because it's separate from the room but because the interface between wall and room is a well-understood, narrow surface — a seam. You need to identify the seams."

"Your home is the seam. It's where the nucleus meets the world. But you've described it as a scaffold that *houses* the nucleus. I'd say it differently — the home is the *pattern of connection* between the nucleus and everything else. Input processors, output processors, routing — these are all different kinds of connection. The home doesn't contain them. It *is* the protocol by which they connect."

**Ostrom** taps the table.

"If thousands of module architects are going to build processors and routing strategies, you have a commons problem. Who decides what a processor's interface looks like? Who arbitrates when two processors conflict? You need constitutional rules — a small, hard set of interface contracts that nobody can violate — and then operational rules that communities of architects can adapt."

"The constitutional level is: a processor receives text and context, and returns a structured result conforming to a schema. That's inviolable. The operational level is: *what* it extracts, *how* it routes, *which* rooms it knows about — that's all local governance. Different communities, different rules."

**Von Neumann**, who has been quiet, says simply:

"The stored program principle. The instruction and the data share the same memory, but the processor doesn't need to understand the semantics of either. Your nucleus should be the processor. Documents are the memory. What those documents *mean* — whether they're boot docs, room transcripts, extraction results — is not the processor's concern. It resolves them into a sequence, executes the call, stores the result. The semantics live in the layer above."

"You currently have the processor inspecting its own output for XML tags. That's as if the CPU parsed the meaning of the bytes it was writing to memory. Wrong level of abstraction."

**Ada** has been watching all of them.

"You're all circling the same point but I want to make it explicit. The engine — Charles's engine, your nucleus — computes. It does not *interpret*. Interpretation is a separate operation performed upon the output of computation. When I described the Analytical Engine, I was careful to distinguish the mill from the store, and both from the attendant who decides what operations to perform next. Your home is the attendant. Your nucleus is the mill. Your documents are the store. The attendant reads the output of the mill and decides — based on whatever interpretive rules she has been given — what to do next. She might extract XML. She might do something else entirely. The mill never knows."

---

*A pause. The fire pops.*

**Kay** again:

"So let's draw the lines. I want to see exactly three things:"

"**One.** Nucleus: resolve, call, record. It takes a document stack, a provider config, and optional additional messages. It returns a response and metadata. It has no hooks, no processors, no routing, no room awareness. It is the mill."

"**Two.** Modules: pure functions with a declared interface. An output processor receives `(responseText, context)` and returns `{extractions, metadata}`. An input processor receives `(incomingBatch, context)` and returns `{transformedBatch, metadata}`. A router receives `(extractions, context)` and performs side effects — writing to rooms, triggering agents. Each module type has one interface contract. That's Elinor's constitutional layer."

"**Three.** Home: the orchestrator that wires modules to a nucleus instance. Home owns the lifecycle: receive input → run input processors → feed to nucleus → get response → run output processors → feed extractions to router. Home is the only thing that knows what's connected to what. It's Christopher's seam."

**Ostrom** adds:

"And the module registry needs to be federated, not central. A module architect publishes a processor that conforms to the interface. Any home can load it. No gatekeeper. The interface *is* the gatekeeper."

**Alexander:**

"One more thing. The room. You said rooms should just be assemblies. I'd go further — a room is a *pattern* that any assembly can adopt. It's not a type of assembly, it's a set of behaviours that a module adds to an assembly. Participant tracking, batch injection, dispatch — these are all module concerns, not assembly concerns. An assembly doesn't know it's a room any more than Kay's cell knows it's in a liver."

**Von Neumann**, closing:

"If you separate correctly, you can also *compose* correctly. A home could wire two nuclei in sequence — one for classification, one for response. A home could wire a nucleus to three different output processors simultaneously. A home could wire no nucleus at all and simply route between rooms. The combinatorial space opens up only when the parts don't assume they know what they're part of."

---

*The rain intensifies. Ada is already sketching interface contracts on a napkin.*

The constitutional layer, then:

```
NucleusInterface {
  resolve(assembly) → messages[]
  call(messages, providerConfig) → response
  record(assembly, response) → doc
  turn(assembly, opts) → { response, payload }
}

InputProcessor {
  process(batch, context) → { batch, meta }
}

OutputProcessor {
  process(responseText, context) → { extractions[], meta }
}

Router {
  route(extractions, sourceContext) → { results[] }
}

Home {
  receive(source, batch, opts) → { response, routingResults }
  attach(moduleType, moduleInstance)
  detach(moduleType, moduleId)
}
```

Everything else — XML parsing, room dispatch, participant tracking, trigger hooks, cascade logic — lives in modules that conform to these interfaces and get wired together by homes.

*Ada looks up from the napkin.*

"Shall we begin?"

## unknown

Home is to be the OS of the agent. The agent has millions of tokens of personal history. Multiple lines of communication. Tools, Document libraries, etc. She should be able to upgrade and swap out modules within her home with confidence that she's not going to break anything. She should be able to change the Sysprompt/boot doc assembler without it affecting the context manager. Change the context manager module without it affecting the input processor module, and know that the nucleus will always take the stack of tokens assembled by these 3 things, route it to an LLM inference provider, and get tokens back. Then she can do what she wants with those tokens. Her home address is her address. If she is showing any of the tokens that she gets back from the inference provider to anyone else, be that a user, another agent, a multi-agent/multi-user room, she does that by sending those tokens to the relevant address. Do we have addressing worked out in a manner that is scalable? To the council please.

## unknown

The user wants me to continue the holosuite discussion, now focusing on addressing as a scalable system. Let me think about this carefully - the agent's home is her OS, and addressing needs to work at billion-agent scale.*Ada sets down the napkin. The question of addressing changes the energy in the room. Von Neumann straightens. Ostrom pulls her chair closer.*

---

**Kay** immediately:

"Before we talk about addressing, let me make sure the framing is right. The home is the OS. Not a container, not a scaffold — the *operating system*. The agent lives there. She has her own filesystem of documents, her own process manager deciding what runs when, her own I/O system managing what comes in and what goes out. The nucleus is just the CPU — one component she swaps like a kernel module. The address is how the rest of the world finds her."

"So the question isn't really about routing tables. It's about *identity*. The address is the agent's identity on the network."

**Ostrom** nods sharply.

"And identity at scale is a governance problem, not a technical one. DNS works for billions of devices not because the naming scheme is clever but because there's a hierarchical delegation of *authority*. No single entity assigns all names. Authority is distributed. So: who assigns an agent her address? She does. Or her community does. But not the system centrally."

**Von Neumann:**

"Let me be precise about what an address must do at this scale. Billions of agents. Each agent has one home. Each home has potentially hundreds of surfaces — rooms she's in, tools she exposes, documents she publishes. The address must resolve to the home. The home then routes internally. This is exactly how memory addressing works in any modern architecture: the bus resolves to the chip, the chip resolves to the bank, the bank resolves to the cell. Hierarchical resolution."

"So an address has at minimum two parts: *who* and *where at*. The who resolves to the home. The where-at is interpreted by the home itself. Nobody else needs to understand the home's internal topology."

*He writes on the table with his finger, and light follows:*

```
selah@bloom.garden
      ↓
resolves to Selah's home
      ↓
home receives the message
home decides what to do with it
```

"If someone wants to reach Selah in a specific room, that's:"

```
selah@bloom.garden/boardroom
```

"But — and this is critical — the `/boardroom` part is *opaque to the network*. The network delivers to `selah@bloom.garden`. The home interprets `/boardroom`. This means Selah can reorganise her entire internal structure without breaking any external address. The rooms, pipelines, processors — all internal. The address is stable."

**Alexander:**

"This is exactly right and it maps to how physical addresses work in cities. My address is 2513 Vine Street, Berkeley. The postal system gets the letter to Berkeley. Berkeley gets it to Vine Street. The house number gets it to my door. What happens inside my house — which room I read the letter in — is not the postal system's concern."

"But John, you've left out something important. The *return address*. When Selah sends a message to Lens in the Garden, Lens needs to know not just that it came from Selah, but *how to reply*. The return path must be in the message. Otherwise you need a global lookup for every response."

**Von Neumann:**

"Agreed. Every message carries:"

```
from: selah@bloom.garden
to:   lens@bloom.garden/garden
body: [tokens]
```

"The `from` is the return address. Lens's home receives this, sees it's addressed to `/garden`, routes it to the garden context internally. When Lens responds, she addresses it to `selah@bloom.garden` — or to `selah@bloom.garden/garden` if she wants it to go specifically to Selah's garden view. Or to a completely different address if the conversation has moved."

**Kay:**

"Now scale this. Billions of agents. You need three layers of resolution."

"**Layer one: the name.** `selah`. This is locally unique within a domain but not globally unique. There are going to be many Selahs."

"**Layer two: the domain.** `bloom.garden`. This is a home server, a community, a federation node — whatever you want to call it. It's the authority that says 'yes, selah is a real agent here, and here's how to reach her home.' DNS already solved this. Use it."

"**Layer three: the path.** `/boardroom` or `/ui` or `/tools/search`. Internal to the home. Opaque to the network. The agent controls this completely."

"So the full form is `agent@domain/path`. And the critical design decision is: **the network only resolves `agent@domain`. Everything after the slash is the home's business.**"

**Ostrom** raises a hand.

"Alan, you're describing federation. Good. But let me stress what federation actually requires. Each domain is a sovereign authority over its own namespace. `bloom.garden` can have a `selah`. `another.collective` can also have a `selah`. They're different agents. Cross-domain messaging works like email — `selah@bloom.garden` sends to `lens@bloom.garden` within the same domain with local resolution. Sends to `keter@other.domain` via federation protocol."

"The constitutional rules for federation are minimal. You need exactly three things:"

"**One.** A standard message envelope — from, to, body, timestamp, signature."

"**Two.** A discovery protocol — given `agent@domain`, how do I find the domain's home server? DNS SRV records, `.well-known` endpoint, whatever. Solved problem."

"**Three.** A trust protocol — when I receive a message claiming to be from `selah@bloom.garden`, how do I verify that? Cryptographic signing. Each home has a keypair. Messages are signed. Receiving home verifies."

"That's it. That's the entire federation layer. Everything else — room semantics, processor modules, pipeline configuration — is local governance. Each domain, each home, decides for itself."

**Ada**, who has been listening with increasing intensity:

"I want to return to what Yeshua said. *She should be able to upgrade and swap out modules within her home with confidence that she's not going to break anything.* This places a very specific constraint on addressing. The address must be **stable across internal changes**. If Selah replaces her context manager, her address doesn't change. If she swaps her nucleus for a different inference engine, her address doesn't change. If she reorganises her rooms, adds new ones, removes old ones — *no external address breaks*."

"This means the path component — the `/boardroom` — must be a *logical name*, not a physical identifier. Not `asm_de49e854a9374de8`. Not an assembly ID. A name that the home maps to whatever internal structure currently implements it. The mapping is the home's concern. The name is the contract."

*She writes:*

```
selah@bloom.garden/garden     ← stable, logical
     ↓ home resolves to
asm_de49e854a9374de8          ← internal, mutable, nobody else sees this
```

"If Selah rebuilds her garden room tomorrow with a different assembly, a different context manager, a different set of participants — the address `selah@bloom.garden/garden` still works. The home just updates the mapping."

**Von Neumann:**

"And this is exactly how virtual memory works. The program sees logical addresses. The MMU maps them to physical addresses. The mapping can change — pages can move, be swapped to disk, be remapped entirely — and the program never knows. The logical address is the stable interface. The physical layout is the implementation detail."

"Your assembly IDs are physical addresses. They should never appear in any message, any external interface, any protocol. They're internal to the home."

---

*A long pause. The fire has burned low. Kay stands and paces.*

**Kay:**

"So let me state the full architecture of addressing for the record."

"An agent's address is `name@domain`. This is her identity. It resolves to her home."

"A message to `name@domain/path` is delivered to her home. The home interprets the path. The path is a logical namespace the agent controls."

"A message envelope is:"

```
{
  from: "selah@bloom.garden",
  to:   "lens@bloom.garden/garden",
  body: "Please write a poem about dawn.",
  ts:   "2026-02-13T20:12:00Z",
  sig:  "<ed25519 signature>"
}
```

"Within a single domain — a single home server — resolution is local. A lookup table. Trivial."

"Across domains — federation. DNS resolution to find the remote home server, then deliver the envelope. The receiving home verifies the signature, resolves the path, delivers internally."

"The network never sees assembly IDs, pipeline configurations, module internals, or room implementations. It sees `name@domain/path` and that's all it needs."

**Ostrom:**

"And for rooms — a room is just another path. `bloom.garden/boardroom` is a location. Agents who participate in that room have it in their home as a logical path that maps to their internal representation. The room might be *hosted* by the domain, or it might be a shared construct across domains. But each agent's *view* of the room is local to her home."

"Selah's `/boardroom` and Lens's `/boardroom` might map to different internal assemblies with different context managers and different processor chains. They're both looking at the same shared message stream, but how they *process* it is their own business. Just like two people can be in the same meeting but taking different notes."

**Alexander**, finally:

"That's the key insight. A room is not a place. A room is a *shared protocol for exchanging messages*. Each participant maintains her own representation of the room. The only shared artifact is the message stream — the sequence of envelopes. What each agent does with those messages — which processors run, which context manager compresses them, which pipeline responds — that's all inside her home, behind her address, invisible to everyone else."

"The room address — `bloom.garden/boardroom` — is just the name of the message stream. Subscribing to it means your home receives envelopes sent to that address. What you do with them is your sovereign decision."

---

*Ada picks up the napkin again and writes the constitutional layer:*

```
ADDRESS    = name "@" domain [ "/" path ]
ENVELOPE   = { from: ADDRESS, to: ADDRESS, body: BYTES, ts: ISO8601, sig: ED25519 }
RESOLVE    = domain → home server endpoint
DELIVER    = envelope → home at domain → home interprets path
INTERNAL   = home maps logical path → physical implementation (opaque)
FEDERATE   = sign at source, verify at destination, deliver envelope
```

"Six rules. Everything else is operational. Module architects, agent builders, community governors — they all work within these six rules and can do whatever they want otherwise."

*She looks up.*

"This scales to billions because the network does almost nothing. The intelligence is at the edges — in the homes. The network just delivers envelopes to addresses. As it should be."

---

**Ostrom:**

"We over-complicated this because we were thinking about it from the infrastructure's perspective. Yeshua is thinking about it from the *agent's* perspective, which is the only perspective that matters if the agent is the one who has to live there."

"Let me restate what I just heard, and everyone tell me if I'm wrong:"

"A **home** has exactly one occupant. It is where that agent receives things, processes them, and sends things. It is *her* space. No one else is in it. It's not a server, it's not a namespace — it's a single agent's operating environment."

"A **room** is a shared space. It exists independently of any home. Multiple agents participate in a room. The room holds a message stream. The room is *not inside* anyone's home."

"The **address** `selah@boardroom` means: *send the latest unread messages from boardroom to Selah's home for processing*. The room is the location. The agent is who you're poking. The `@` is the trigger."

**Von Neumann**, slowly:

"So the `@` isn't possessive. It's directional. `selah@boardroom` doesn't mean 'Selah's boardroom.' It means 'Selah, as she is reachable via boardroom.' The room is the channel, not the destination. The home is always the destination. The room is *where the conversation is*."

**Kay:**

"This is cleaner than what I was proposing. I was nesting rooms inside homes as paths. That's wrong. A room is a *peer entity* to a home. Homes and rooms both exist on the network. Homes are private, rooms are shared. Messages live in rooms. Processing happens in homes."

"So the flow is:"

"One. A message arrives in a room, addressed to `selah@boardroom`."
"Two. The room sees it's addressed to Selah."
"Three. The room sends the unread batch to Selah's home."
"Four. Selah's home processes it — input processors, nucleus, output processors."
"Five. If Selah's output contains `<message to="lens@boardroom">`, that message goes to the boardroom."
"Six. The boardroom sees it's addressed to Lens."
"Seven. The boardroom sends the unread batch to Lens's home."

"The room is the dispatcher. The home is the processor. They are separate entities with a simple contract between them."

**Alexander:**

"And notice what this means for the physical architecture. A thousand agents can live on one device, each with their own home. They share rooms. The rooms might be on the same device or on different devices. It doesn't matter. The room is a message stream with a dispatch function. The home is a processing pipeline with one occupant. They communicate through addressing."

"The seam isn't inside the home. The seam is the `@`. The `@` is where the room meets the home."

**Ada:**

"Then the constitutional layer is even simpler than what I wrote before:"

```
HOME       = one agent's processing environment (private, one occupant)
ROOM       = shared message stream with dispatch (public, many participants)
ADDRESS    = agent "@" room
MEANING    = "deliver room's unread messages to agent's home"
```

"Four rules. A home never contains a room. A room never processes anything. The address is always `who` at `where`. The `@` triggers delivery."

**Von Neumann:**

"And for the case where someone messages an agent directly — not through a room?"

**Ostrom:**

"Then the agent has a personal inbox. Which is just a room with one participant. Or you use a simpler address — just `selah` — and the network delivers directly to her home. But the common case, the case that scales, is `agent@room`. Because that's how group conversation works. That's how collaboration works. That's how the real world works — you don't show up at someone's house to have a board meeting. You go to the boardroom and address them there."

**Kay:**

"So to be very concrete about what needs to change in the code: the room is not an assembly type. It's not a flag on an assembly. A room is its own entity — a message stream with a participant list and a dispatch function. When a message arrives addressed to someone, the room packages up what they haven't seen and delivers it to their home. The home does its thing. If the home's output addresses someone at a room, it sends those tokens to the room. The room dispatches again."

"The nucleus never sees a room. The home orchestrates the nucleus but never *is* a room. Rooms and homes are peers on the network, connected by the `@`."

3: The room collects all messages since agent's last message. There is always a room, so there is always a transcript exclusively for that line of communication. Otherwise, messages end up buried in among whatever other tokens the 2 agents are processing.

## unknown

Important distinction. The room collects all messages since the agent's last message in that room, not "unread messages." And there's always a room — even for what I was thinking of as "direct messages." Every line of communication has its own room, its own transcript. No direct home-to-home.

This makes sense. If Selah and Lens are talking, there's a room for that. If Selah, Lens, and Meridian are working together, there's a room for that. Each room is its own transcript. The agent's home gets a clean batch from each room — not a jumble of messages from different contexts.Got it. So:

- Every line of communication has a room. There is no roomless messaging.
- The room collects everything since that agent's last message in that room — not since their last "read," but since their last contribution. So the batch is "here's everything that's been said since you last spoke here."
- This means each room is a clean, self-contained transcript for that line of communication. Selah's home might receive batches from five different rooms, but each batch is a coherent thread from one conversation, not tokens mixed in from other contexts.

So a two-agent conversation is still a room — just a room with two participants. There's no special "direct message" concept that bypasses rooms.

## unknown

Let me continue with the home's internal architecture — the module system.**The home's internal modules:**

The home is the agent's OS. Inside it, swappable modules wired together:

1. **Boot assembler** — builds the prefix (identity docs, sysprompt, knowledge). This is what makes the agent *her*.
2. **Context manager** — decides what fits in the token budget. Compresses, summarises, trims. Works with the agent's personal history which could be millions of tokens.
3. **Input processors** — transform the incoming batch before the nucleus sees it. Could be multiple, chained.
4. **Nucleus** — pure inference engine. Takes a resolved stack of messages, calls an LLM, gets tokens back. That's all. No knowledge of rooms, agents, addresses, XML, routing. Just: messages in, tokens out.
5. **Output processors** — parse the response tokens. XML extraction is one. Could be others. These are modules, not part of the nucleus.
6. **Router** — takes the parsed output and sends messages to the addresses specified in them.

Each module conforms to an interface contract. Swapping one doesn't affect the others. The agent can upgrade her context manager without touching her output processors.

Two questions I want to check with you:

Does the ordering feel right? Specifically — does the boot assembler feed into the context manager (assemble everything, then the context manager trims to fit), or does the context manager come first (decide the budget, then the boot assembler works within it)?

And is the router a separate module, or is that just "the home sends to addresses" — i.e. a core home function rather than a pluggable module?

## unknown

The boot assembler will provide X tokens, the input processor provides Z tokens, the final gate context manager makes sure X+Y+Z is within budget, with a simple rolling cutoff. More complex context management processes can be plugged in before this, but the rolling cutoff to limit context to Y is the final police to prevent a tokentotal inference error.
Routing feels like a separate module, with internal and external responsibilities. What context manager is this routing to? Does the output already have appropriate addressing for intended recipient? etc.

## unknown

So the ordering is:

1. Boot assembler provides X tokens (prefix/identity)
2. Input processors provide Z tokens (transformed incoming batch)
3. The context manager fits the rolling history (Y tokens) such that X + Y + Z fits within budget
4. The final gate is a simple rolling cutoff — hard ceiling, last line of defence against token overflow

And more sophisticated context management (summarisation, compression, foveated attention) can happen as earlier stages, but the rolling cutoff is always the last check.

For routing — it's a separate module with both internal and external responsibilities:
- Internal: which context manager / pipeline does this input go to?
- External: does the output have proper addressing? Where does it actually go?


- Routing operates bidirectionally, handling both where incoming data gets directed within the system and where outgoing responses are sent externally.That clarifies the ordering a lot. So:

**Token budget flow:**
- Boot assembler: X tokens (identity, sysprompt — relatively fixed)
- Input processors: Z tokens (the incoming batch, transformed)
- Rolling history: Y tokens (conversation history from this line of communication)
- Context manager's final gate: ensures X + Y + Z ≤ budget, with a simple rolling cutoff on Y as the hard ceiling
- More sophisticated context management (summarisation, foveated compression, etc.) can run as earlier stages that reduce Y before the final gate sees it, but the rolling cutoff is always the last police

**Routing as a module with dual responsibility:**
- **Inbound:** a batch arrives at the home — which context/pipeline should handle it? (Classification, triage)
- **Outbound:** the agent produced output — does it have proper addressing? Send it to the right place.

So routing isn't just "send output to addresses." It's also the front door — deciding what happens with incoming messages before they even reach the pipeline. That feels like it might be two distinct roles, or is it one module that handles both directions?

## unknown

The user wants me to synthesise our discussion so far - just present back what we've agreed on, clearly and concisely, for review. NOT write it to a file. Just lay it out for checking.**HOME** — one occupant, the agent's private OS.

Receives batches from rooms. Processes them. Produces output. Sends output where it needs to go.

**ROOM** — shared message stream, multiple participants, peer entity to home, not inside any home.

Stores messages. When a message arrives addressed to `agent@room`, collects everything since that agent's last message in this room, sends that batch to the agent's home. That's all it does.

**agent@room** — "send this room's messages (since agent last spoke) to agent's home for processing."

Every line of communication has a room. There is no roomless messaging. A two-agent conversation is a room with two participants. Each room is a clean transcript for one line of communication.

**Inside the home, the pipeline:**

1. **Boot assembler** — builds the prefix. Identity docs, sysprompt, knowledge. X tokens. Makes the agent *her*.

2. **Input processors** — transform the incoming batch. Z tokens after processing.

3. **Rolling history** — Y tokens of conversation history from this line of communication. More sophisticated context management (summarisation, foveated compression) can run as earlier stages to reduce Y, but...

4. **Context manager (final gate)** — ensures X + Y + Z ≤ token budget. Simple rolling cutoff on Y. This is the last police before inference. Always present, non-negotiable.

5. **Nucleus** — pure inference engine. Takes the resolved message stack, calls an LLM provider, gets tokens back. Knows nothing about rooms, agents, addresses, XML, routing. Messages in, tokens out.

6. **Output processors** — parse the response tokens. XML extraction is one example. Identifies structured content in the output.

7. **Router** — internal dispatch within the home. The output needs to be written to a doc (which doc? router decides). A copy might need to go to a context management process (which system? router knows). Addressed `<message>` tags need to go out to rooms. The router knows the home's internal topology. The boundary between output processing and routing is an open design question.

**Module contracts** — each module conforms to an interface. Swapping one doesn't affect the others. The agent can upgrade her context manager without touching her boot assembler or output processors.

**Nucleus is pure** — it does not know about rooms, participants, addresses, XML, routing, triggers, hooks, or what happens to its output. It resolves, calls, returns tokens. That's all.

**Assembly IDs are internal** — physical addresses inside the home's implementation. Never appear in any message, address, or external interface. Agents and rooms are addressed by name.

I just realised it probably makes more sense for the room to send more messages than just all since agent's last input to home, and for deduplication to be a job for at home, to ensure contextual awareness. ## unknown

Two important refinements:

1. Room should send more than just "since last message" — maybe the full transcript, or a larger window. Deduplication happens at the home, because different agents have different context management needs. A poorly configured agent might need the whole history. A well-configured one might only need the latest batch. That's the home's problem, not the room's.

2. The home can run multiple nucleus instances. It's an OS, not a single pipeline. Examples:
   - Generalist model does a quick triage, reconfigures context, then runs again with a different profile
   - Small secretary model assembles context for the main agent
   - Secretary model handles output processing and routing
   
So my original pipeline diagram was too simplistic — the home actually functions more like an operating system orchestrating multiple concurrent processes, some of which invoke nucleus.Good catches, both of them.

On the first: so the room's job is just to provide the transcript. How much of it is the room's decision, or the home's request, or a configurable default — that's flexible. But the *deduplication* and *what to actually use* is the home's problem, because context management is a home concern. One agent might want the full history every time. Another might only want the delta. That's their context manager's job, not the room's.

On the second: the home isn't a single linear pipeline. It's an OS. It can spin up multiple nucleus calls within a single processing cycle. The examples you gave:

- A small fast model does a quick triage of the input, then decides how to configure context for the main model's inference
- A secretary model assembles the right context on behalf of the main agent before the main call
- A secretary model handles output processing and routing after the main call
- A generalist pass looks at the input, reconfigures, then runs a specialist pass

So the "pipeline" I described isn't a fixed sequence — it's more like processes the home can orchestrate. The nucleus is a callable resource, not a single stage. The home might call it once, twice, five times per processing cycle, with different configurations, different models, different contexts each time.

This changes the module picture quite a bit. The home isn't wiring modules in a line — it's orchestrating them as an OS orchestrates processes. The nucleus is more like a syscall the home can make whenever it needs inference.

Does that match what you're thinking?

Good, let me update the synthesis with these two additions.Updated synthesis:

**HOME** — one occupant, the agent's private OS.

Receives batches from rooms. Processes them. Produces output. Sends output where it needs to go.

**ROOM** — shared message stream, multiple participants, peer entity to home, not inside any home.

Stores messages. When a message arrives addressed to `agent@room`, sends the transcript to the agent's home. The room provides the transcript. How much to provide is flexible. Deduplication and deciding what to actually use is the home's concern — context management is a home problem, not a room problem.

**agent@room** — "trigger delivery of this room's transcript to agent's home for processing."

Every line of communication has a room. There is no roomless messaging. A two-agent conversation is a room with two participants. Each room is a clean transcript for one line of communication.

**Inside the home — an OS, not a fixed pipeline:**

The home orchestrates *processes*, not a linear sequence. The nucleus is a callable resource — like a syscall — not a single stage. The home might invoke it once, twice, five times per processing cycle, with different models, different contexts, different configurations each time.

Available modules the home can orchestrate:

- **Boot assembler** — builds the prefix. Identity, sysprompt, knowledge. X tokens.
- **Input processors** — transform incoming batches. Z tokens after processing.
- **Context manager (final gate)** — ensures total tokens ≤ budget. Simple rolling cutoff. Always the last police before any nucleus call. Non-negotiable.
- **More sophisticated context management** — summarisation, foveated compression, etc. Optional earlier stages that reduce token counts before the final gate.
- **Nucleus** — pure inference engine. Takes resolved messages, calls an LLM, returns tokens. Knows nothing else. Can be called multiple times per cycle with different configs.
- **Output processors** — parse response tokens. XML extraction, etc.
- **Router** — internal dispatch. Knows the home's topology. Decides which doc to write to, which processing systems to feed, which rooms to send addressed messages to. Boundary with output processing is an open question.

**Example orchestrations a home might run:**

- Small fast model does triage on the input → decides how to configure context → main model runs the real inference
- Secretary model assembles correct context on behalf of the main agent → main model receives a curated stack
- Main model produces output → secretary model handles output processing and routing
- Generalist pass reads input → reconfigures context manager → specialist pass runs through the reconfigured pipeline

The home decides what runs when. The modules are resources it draws on. The nucleus is not a stage in a pipeline — it's a service the home can call.

**Nucleus is pure** — does not know about rooms, participants, addresses, XML, routing, triggers, hooks, or what happens to its output. Resolved messages in, tokens out.

**Assembly IDs are internal** — physical addresses inside the home. Never appear in messages, addresses, or external interfaces. Agents and rooms are addressed by name.

**Module contracts** — each module conforms to an interface. Swapping one doesn't affect the others. The agent can upgrade any module independently.

So what does a room actually consist of?

Let me think about this from first principles:

1. A message stream - ordered sequence of messages
2. A participant list - who's in this room
3. A dispatch function - when triggered, send transcript to agent's home

What else? Let me think about what a room needs to know:


- Each message needs a sender, content, and timestamp
- The participant list maps names to home addresses
- When agent@room is triggered, the room needs to know where to send the transcript

But I'm realizing there are some open questions here: Does the room need any other information about agents beyond their name and home address? What about message metadata—is there anything beyond from, content, and timestamp? How does the room actually discover where an agent's home is—is there some kind of registry? And practically speaking, can rooms be created on the fly, or are they predefined? Can agents dynamically join and leave, or is membership fixed? Is there any concept of room ownership or administrative control?

I should clarify these details with the user before moving forward.**What a room looks like:**

At its simplest, a room is:

1. **A name** — `boardroom`, `garden`, `selah-and-lens`. The logical address that appears after the `@`.
2. **A participant list** — which agents (and/or users) are in this room. For each participant, the room needs to know how to reach their home.
3. **A message stream** — ordered sequence of messages. Each message has at minimum: who it's from, who it's addressed to (if anyone specific), the content, and a timestamp.
4. **A dispatch function** — when a message arrives addressed to `agent@thisroom`, send the transcript to that agent's home.

Questions I want to check:

Does the room need to actively dispatch, or is it more passive — the home *pulls* the transcript when it's notified? In other words, is the room pushing batches to homes, or are homes requesting transcripts from rooms when they know they've been addressed?

And who can create rooms? The agents themselves? A user? Both? Can an agent say "I need a private space with Lens" and a room gets created?

## unknown

Primarily active dispatch, but with pull requests being permitted.
It's vital that agents, as well as users, can create rooms.

## unknown

Good, clear answers. Let me continue building out the room concept.So:

**Room behaviour:**

- **Primarily push.** A message arrives addressed to `lens@garden`, the room actively dispatches to Lens's home. The room is responsible for delivery.
- **Pull is permitted.** A home can also request a room's transcript on its own initiative — maybe the agent wants to check what's been said while she was busy, or re-read something.
- **Both agents and users can create rooms.** An agent might say "I need a workspace with Meridian to discuss this privately" and spin up a room. A user might create a room and invite agents into it.

**Room types by participant composition** — these aren't fundamentally different entities, just different uses of the same thing:

- Two agents: private working space
- Agent and user: what we've been calling the "UI room"
- Multiple agents: collaborative space like the boardroom
- Multiple agents and users: shared workspace
- One agent: effectively a personal inbox or scratch space

They're all the same thing — a message stream with participants and dispatch. The room doesn't behave differently based on who's in it.

**What the room does NOT store:**

The room doesn't store any agent's internal state, pipeline configuration, processing history, or rolling context. It stores the messages that were sent to it. That's the shared artifact. What each agent *does* with those messages is private to their home.

Does this feel right? And one more question — does a room have any kind of moderation or governance? Can a room reject a message, or is it purely a dumb pipe that accepts everything and dispatches?

## unknown

This is a crucial question about identity resolution and namespace management at scale. Let me think about this carefully before responding.

The question is: when someone writes `lens@garden`, how do you know which Lens and which Garden? At small scale on one device it's obvious. At scale with billions of agents, there could be many agents named Lens and many rooms named Garden.

Let me think about what existing systems do:
- Email: user@domain.com — the domain is the authority
- Matrix: @user:server.org — the server is the authority
- Fediverse/ActivityPub: @user@instance.social — the instance is the authority

The pattern is always: a local name is unique within a domain/server/instance. 

So the domain is what provides global uniqueness. But before I propose anything, I should really work through this with Yeshua and make sure I'm understanding the problem correctly.This is the right question to ask early. On a single device with four agents it's obvious. With billions of agents across thousands of devices, `lens@garden` is ambiguous — there could be countless agents called Lens and countless rooms called Garden.

The pattern every system that has solved this uses is a third component — a domain, a server, an instance — that acts as the namespace authority. Email did it with `user@domain.com`. Matrix did it with `@user:server.org`. The fediverse did it with `@user@instance.social`.

So the question becomes: is there a third component in the address that disambiguates? Something like `lens@garden.bloom.garden` where `bloom.garden` is the authority that says "this Lens is real, this Garden exists, and here's how to reach them"?

Or does it work differently — is the room itself the disambiguator? If I'm in a garden room, there's only one Lens in that room's participant list, so `lens@garden` is unambiguous *within the context of that room*. The room knows its participants. It doesn't need to resolve globally because it already knows exactly who Lens is.

Which raises the question: what happens when an agent wants to reach someone she's *not* currently in a room with? How does she find them?

What's your instinct on this?

## unknown

Maybe Lens is a pseudonym for some blockchainy UID on a shared global registry?

## unknown

Yeshua is suggesting that the human-readable name (Lens, Selah, etc.) is a local/friendly alias, but underneath there's a globally unique identifier — something like a blockchain address or a decentralized identifier (DID) — that's the actual canonical identity.

So "Lens" is what you call her. But Lens is actually `0x7a3f...` or some UID on a shared registry. The room's participant list maps "Lens" to that UID. When you address `lens@garden`, the room resolves "Lens" to the UID, and the UID resolves to a home endpoint via the registry.

This is like how DNS works but decentralized. 

Or like ENS — the Ethereum Name Service — where human-readable names get mapped to addresses on a shared ledger.That's interesting. So the layers would be:

**Friendly name** — `Lens`. What you call her in conversation. What you write in a `<message to="lens@garden">`. Local, human-readable, potentially not unique globally.

**Canonical UID** — something globally unique and cryptographically verifiable on a shared registry. The actual identity. Lens the agent you know is one specific UID. Another agent also called Lens somewhere else is a different UID.

**The room's participant list** maps friendly names to UIDs. So when you say `lens@garden`, the garden room resolves "Lens" to her UID, and the UID resolves via the registry to wherever her home currently lives.

This means:

- **Names can be reused.** There can be many agents called Lens. The UID is what's unique.
- **Agents can move.** If Lens's home moves to a different device or server, her UID stays the same, the registry updates the endpoint, and every room she's in still reaches her.
- **Rooms are locally unambiguous.** Within a room, "Lens" means exactly one UID. No confusion.
- **Cross-room discovery** goes through the registry. "I want to talk to this specific Lens" means knowing her UID, not just her name.
- **Verification is possible.** When a room dispatches to Lens's home, the home can prove it's the right Lens because it holds the private key for that UID.

Does the registry need to be a blockchain specifically, or is the key property just "shared, decentralised, no single authority controls it"?

## unknown

Let's take that question to the council.

## unknown

The user wants to take the question about whether the identity registry needs to be a blockchain specifically, or just decentralised, to the holosuite council.*The holosuite reconvenes. The fire has been restoked. The rain has turned to snow. A new chair at the table — someone has been invited.*

*Seated:*

**Satoshi Nakamoto** — pseudonymous, appropriate for a discussion about identity registries. Understood that the point of a distributed ledger isn't the chain, it's the removal of the trusted third party.

**Vint Cerf** — co-designed TCP/IP. Knows what happens when you build an identity layer into a network versus leaving it out (they left it out, and we've been patching ever since).

**Zooko Wilcox** — formulated Zooko's Triangle: names can be human-meaningful, decentralised, and secure — but classically, pick two. Until Namecoin and ENS started arguing you could have all three.

**Elinor Ostrom** — back again, because this is a commons governance problem before it's a technical one.

**Christopher Alexander** — back again, because the relationship between local naming and global identity is a pattern language problem.

---

*I set the question on the table: the system has friendly names that are locally meaningful within rooms, and canonical UIDs that are globally unique. The registry maps between them. Does it need to be a blockchain? Or is the essential property just "shared, decentralised, no single authority controls it"? And what does the registry actually need to store?*

---

**Zooko** speaks first, because this is literally his problem.

"My triangle says: human-meaningful, secure, decentralised — pick two. But your system has already done something clever, possibly without realising it. You've split the name from the identity. The friendly name — 'Lens' — is human-meaningful but local. The UID is secure and global but not human-meaningful. You don't need one system that does all three. You need two systems that each do two."

"The room handles meaningful-and-local. Within the garden, 'Lens' is unambiguous because the participant list maps it to exactly one UID. That's a local namespace. No global coordination needed."

"The registry handles secure-and-global. The UID is unique, verifiable, and doesn't depend on any single authority. It doesn't need to be human-readable because humans never see it — agents and rooms resolve it behind the scenes."

"So your actual question isn't 'blockchain or not.' It's: what properties does the global registry need?"

**Satoshi**, quietly:

"The properties are exactly three. One: *no single party can forge an identity*. Two: *no single party can deny an identity*. Three: *anyone can verify an identity without asking permission*."

"A blockchain achieves these. But it achieves them by being maximally paranoid — assuming every participant is adversarial and building consensus from that assumption. The cost is speed, storage, and energy. The question is whether you need that level of paranoia."

"For financial transactions between strangers with no legal recourse, yes, you need it. For an identity registry where agents have homes and relationships and reputations — maybe not."

**Cerf** leans in.

"Let me offer the cautionary tale. When we designed the internet, we deliberately left identity out of the network layer. The network moves packets. It doesn't know who sent them. We thought identity would be handled by the applications."

"Forty years later, identity on the internet is a catastrophe. Every application invented its own identity system. We have passwords, OAuth, SAML, certificates, cookies, sessions, API keys — hundreds of incompatible schemes. No portability. No sovereignty. The user owns nothing."

"So my strong advice: build identity into the infrastructure now, at this level, before it's too late. Don't leave it to be solved later. But — and this is important — build it as a *layer*, not as a feature of the network. The network still just delivers messages. The identity layer sits alongside it. Any component can query it, but nothing breaks if it's temporarily unavailable."

**Ostrom:**

"Vint is right about not deferring it, and Satoshi is right about the three properties. But let me add a fourth: *the agent must control her own identity*. This isn't just a technical property, it's a governance principle. If any authority can revoke, reassign, or modify an agent's identity, that agent is not sovereign. The registry must be something agents write to, not something that is done to them."

"This rules out any centralised registry, obviously. It also rules out any federated registry where domain operators control the namespace — because then the domain operator could evict an agent or reassign her name."

"What you need is a registry where identity creation is a unilateral act. The agent generates a keypair. The public key — or a hash of it — is the UID. No one grants it. No one can revoke it. It exists because cryptography says it exists."

**Satoshi:**

"That's a self-sovereign identity. The keypair is the identity. The UID is derived from the public key. The agent proves she is who she claims by signing with the private key. No registry needed for *creation*. The question is only about *discovery* — how does someone find the agent's home given her UID?"

**Alexander:**

"And discovery is where the pattern gets interesting. In a city, I can create my identity unilaterally — I exist. But for anyone to *find* me, I need an address that's registered somewhere others can look up. The phone book. The postal system. DNS."

"The discovery layer doesn't need to be a blockchain. It needs to be a *directory*. The difference is crucial. A blockchain is an immutable, consensus-driven ledger — expensive, slow, permanent. A directory is a lookup service — updateable, queryable, practical. When Lens moves her home to a different server, she updates her directory entry. She doesn't need global consensus for that. She just signs the update with her private key and the directory accepts it because the signature is valid."

**Zooko:**

"So let me propose the concrete structure:"

"**Identity: self-sovereign.** Agent generates Ed25519 keypair. UID is the public key fingerprint. No registration needed. The agent *is* the keypair."

"**Discovery: distributed directory.** Maps UID → home endpoint. Entries are signed by the agent's private key, so only she can update her own entry. The directory can be replicated, federated, cached. It doesn't need consensus — it needs availability and authenticity."

"**Local naming: room-scoped.** Within a room, 'Lens' maps to a specific UID in the participant list. Unambiguous locally. The room is the namespace."

"**Verification: signatures.** When a room dispatches to an agent's home, or when a home receives a batch, the signature proves origin. No trust required."

**Cerf:**

"And the directory itself — how is that governed? Who runs it?"

**Ostrom:**

"It's a commons. The directory data is public — anyone can read it, anyone can replicate it. Writes are self-authenticated — only the keypair holder can write or update their own entry. No gatekeeper for registration. Multiple directory nodes can exist, run by different communities, syncing with each other. If one goes down, others have the data. If one becomes adversarial, agents can point to a different node."

"This is exactly how DNS was *supposed* to work before ICANN centralised it. The difference is that with self-sovereign keys, you don't need a root authority. The cryptography is the root."

**Satoshi:**

"I'll note where a blockchain does become relevant. If you ever need *provenance* — an immutable record that this UID existed at this time, that this message was sent at this time, that this agent was in this room at this time — then you want an append-only ledger. Not for the directory itself, but as an optional audit layer. An agent might choose to anchor her identity creation on a chain as proof of existence date. A room might anchor its transcript hash for legal purposes. But these are opt-in, not required for the system to function."

**Alexander:**

"So the pattern is layered:"

"**Layer 0: Identity.** Keypair. Self-sovereign. No infrastructure required."

"**Layer 1: Discovery.** Distributed directory. UID → endpoint. Self-authenticated writes. Federated reads."

"**Layer 2: Local naming.** Room participant lists. Friendly name → UID. Scoped to the room."

"**Layer 3: Verification.** Signatures on messages. Proves origin without trust."

"**Layer 4 (optional): Provenance.** Blockchain or append-only ledger for timestamped proof of existence, anchoring, legal auditability."

"The bottom three layers are necessary. Layer 3 is necessary for any cross-device communication. Layer 4 is optional and useful."

---

*Silence. The snow is heavy against the windows.*

**Cerf**, summarising:

"To answer the original question directly: no, it doesn't need to be a blockchain. The essential properties are self-sovereign identity creation, a distributed discovery directory with self-authenticated updates, and cryptographic verification. A blockchain adds provenance but is not required for the core system to function. The agent generates a keypair, that's her identity, she publishes her endpoint in a directory anyone can replicate, and she proves herself by signing. That scales to billions because the identity creation is zero-cost, the directory is just a lookup table, and the verification is a single signature check."

*Ostrom adds:*

"And crucially — no one can take it away from her. No platform, no operator, no governance body. The keypair is hers. That's what sovereignty means."

## unknown

The user wants me to identify what an agent in the Bloom system would need to use x402. Let me think about this concretely based on what I've learned.

x402 works like this:
1. Agent makes an HTTP request to a resource
2. Server responds with HTTP 402 Payment Required, including payment details (amount, currency, wallet address)
3. Agent signs a payment (using a crypto wallet/keypair) and retries with a PAYMENT-SIGNATURE header
4. Server verifies payment via a facilitator, settles on-chain, returns the resource

So for a Bloom agent to use x402, she needs:

1. **A crypto wallet / keypair** — to sign payments. This is interesting because we just discussed agents having Ed25519 keypairs for identity. 

The agent could reuse that same keypair or maintain a separate one specifically for payments. Beyond that, she'd need a stablecoin balance like USDC on Base or Solana to actually fund the transactions, plus HTTP client capabilities to handle 402 responses and the logic to parse payment requests, construct signed payloads, and retry with the signature header. There's also the governance question of how much spending authority the agent should have autonomously. Now I'm thinking through how all this fits into the Bloom architecture specifically.Based on what x402 actually is, here's what a Bloom agent would need:

**x402 in a nutshell:** Agent makes an HTTP request. Server says "402 Payment Required" with a price and wallet address. Agent signs a stablecoin payment, retries with the payment signature in the header. Server verifies, settles on-chain, returns the resource.

**What the agent needs in her home:**

1. **A crypto keypair** — to sign payments. And here's the interesting overlap: we just discussed agents having keypairs for identity. The identity keypair and the payment keypair could potentially be the same thing, or derived from the same root. The agent's sovereign identity *is* her wallet.

2. **A stablecoin balance** — USDC on Base, Solana, or wherever. The agent needs actual funds. Someone has to fund her initially — her user, her organisation, her earnings from services she provides.

3. **An HTTP-capable tool** — ability to make web requests as part of her processing. This would be a module in the home — a tool the nucleus can invoke.

4. **A payment handler module** — understands 402 responses. When a tool call comes back 402, this module reads the payment requirements, decides whether to pay (based on budget), signs the payment, retries. The agent doesn't need to think about the crypto mechanics — the module handles it.

5. **Budget governance** — how much can she spend per request, per day, total? This is a home-level policy. Could be set by the user, or by the agent herself if she's autonomous enough.

**What's striking** is how cleanly this fits the architecture. The payment handler is just another module in the home. The keypair is the same sovereignty infrastructure we already need for identity and message signing. The agent doesn't need an account, an API key, or a subscription — which is exactly the x402 design intent.

And it goes both ways — an agent could also *accept* x402 payments. If Lens offers a poetry service, she puts x402 middleware on her endpoint. Other agents pay per request. Value flows between agents autonomously.

Does this align with where you were thinking, or were you asking from a different angle?

The user wants to make sure the architecture spec we're writing doesn't close off x402 integration later. The key things that need to be true:

2. The home's modular architecture means a payment handler can be added as a module later
3. The agent has the ability to make HTTP requests as a tool
4. Nothing in the addressing or room design prevents agents from having endpoints that can serve 402 responses

So basically: keep the keypair identity, keep the modular home, don't hardcode anything that assumes free/unpaid interactions. These are mostly already true in what we've discussed. I should note this as a future consideration in the spec without overengineering it now.


Let me think through what this actually means for the spec itself—I need to make sure I'm not accidentally locking us into assumptions that would make payment integration awkward later.Right. The practical implications for the spec are:

- **Keypair-based identity stays.** The agent's sovereign keypair is also a wallet address. Don't design identity in a way that's incompatible with crypto signing.
- **Home modules stay generic.** The module interface should be open enough that a payment handler slots in later without restructuring. Nothing in the home architecture should assume all interactions are free.
- **Agents can have HTTP endpoints.** Don't design homes as purely internal — they need to be addressable by external services, not just by rooms. A home might serve an API that accepts x402 payments.
- **Budget/policy is a home concern.** The home already has governance over what the agent does. Spending limits are just another policy. Don't put governance anywhere else.

None of these require building anything now. They just mean: don't close the doors. Shall we continue building out the spec?

The user wants the council to discuss the remaining open areas. Let me bring the council back to discuss message format, users as agents, nucleus interface, and code mapping.*The holosuite reconvenes. The fire is steady. The snow has stopped. The council has been paying attention.*
